% =================================================================
\section{The universal discriminant}
\label{sec:universal-discriminant}
% =================================================================

The discriminant $(1-3x)(1+x)$ governs three families.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{$\widehat{\fsl}_2$ bar cohomology.}\enspace
The Riordan numbers $R(n)$, with generating function
\[
R(x) \;=\; \frac{1+x - \sqrt{(1-3x)(1+x)}}{2x(1+x)}.
\]

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{Virasoro bar cohomology.}\enspace
The Motzkin differences $M(n{+}1) - M(n)$, with generating
function satisfying the cubic
$x^3 P^2 - (1{-}2x)(1{-}x^2)P + (1{-}x)(1{-}x^2) = 0$.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{$\beta\gamma$ bar cohomology.}\enspace
Generating function $\sqrt{(1+x)/(1-3x)}$.

\medskip

Growth rate $3$ for all three.  Convergence radius $1/3$ for
all three.  The discriminant $(1-3x)(1+x) = 1-2x-3x^2$ has roots
at $x = 1/3$ and $x = -1$.

\sep

Why $3$?  The bar differential extracts residues at collisions
using the propagator $\eta_{ij} = d\log(z_i - z_j)$.  At each
collision, a two-pole OPE ($z^{-2}$ and $z^{-1}$) produces
three independent residue extractions: the level term, the
bracket term, and their cross-term.  The growth rate
$3 =$ number of independent residue extractions per step.

Why $-1$?  The bar complex uses desuspension: the Koszul sign.
The $\Z/2$-symmetry of the desuspension contributes a root at
$x = -1$.  The two factors of the discriminant have separate
origins: $(1-3x)$ is the OPE, $(1+x)$ is the grading.

Drinfeld--Sokolov reduction preserves the discriminant:
$\widehat{\fsl}_2$ and Virasoro share $(1-3x)(1+x)$ because DS
is a functor on bar complexes that acts on the generating function
by a rational transformation that preserves the branch locus.
The discriminant is a Koszul invariant.

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lccc@{}}
\textbf{Algebra}
  & \textbf{Discriminant}
  & \textbf{Growth}
  & \textbf{Radius} \\
\hline
$\widehat{\fsl}_2$ & $(1-3x)(1+x)$ & $3^n/n^{3/2}$ & $1/3$ \\
Virasoro & $(1-3x)(1+x)$ & $3^n/n^{3/2}$ & $1/3$ \\
$\beta\gamma$ & $(1-3x)(1+x)$ & $3^n/\sqrt{n}$ & $1/3$ \\
$\widehat{\fsl}_3$ & $1-3x-x^2$ & $\approx 3.3^n$ & $(\sqrt{13}-3)/2$
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip

The $\fsl_3$ discriminant breaks the pattern:
$1 - 3x - x^2$ has roots involving $\sqrt{13}$, and
$13 = \dim(\fsl_3)$.  The recurrence depth of the generating
function equals $\operatorname{rank}(\fg) + 1$ in all computed
cases.


% =================================================================
\section{The depth creation mechanism}
\label{sec:depth-creation}
% =================================================================

Free bosons have $S_4 = 0$.  The shadow obstruction tower terminates at
arity~$2$: class~G.  No quartic shadow, no infinite tower, no
contact invariant.

The Sugawara tensor $T = \sum_a \normord{\partial\phi_a
\partial\phi_a}/(2k)$ is quadratic in the free fields.  Its
shadow obstruction tower inherits $S_4$ from cross-correlators between
distinct bosons:
\[
S_4(T) \;=\; \frac{1}{\kappa(T)}
\sum_{a \neq b} \langle \partial\phi_a\, \partial\phi_a\,
\partial\phi_b\, \partial\phi_b \rangle_{\mathrm{conn}}.
\]
This is nonzero because the Sugawara construction mixes the
bosons.  The quadratic nonlinearity creates the quartic from
nothing.

Three views of the same phenomenon.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{(i) The HPL view.}\enspace
The Heisenberg--plus--Lie decomposition of the affine OPE
separates into a Heisenberg part ($k\kappa^{ab}/(z-w)^2$)
and a Lie part ($f^{ab}{}_c J^c/(z-w)$).  After DS reduction,
the ghost-number obstruction kills the coproduct of the
Heisenberg part; the $r$-matrix of the Lie part survives.  The
quartic shadow is the first nontrivial interaction between
Heisenberg and Lie data in the reduced algebra.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{(ii) The spectral sequence view.}\enspace
The PBW spectral sequence at $E_1$ contains the affine
generators.  The $d_1$ differential kills them: at $E_2$, only
Virasoro and higher-spin composites survive.  These composites
carry quartic poles by double Wick contraction.  The spectral
sequence is the mechanism by which a $2$-pole algebra becomes
a $4$-pole algebra.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{(iii) The Miura view.}\enspace
$T = \sum (\partial\phi)^2$ creates quartic from Gaussian
cross-correlators: $\langle (\partial\phi_a)^2
(\partial\phi_b)^2 \rangle_{\mathrm{conn}} = 2\delta_{ab}$, but
the sum over $a \neq b$ produces a genuinely quartic contribution
to the contact invariant.

\sep

The depth increases from class~L to class~M.  The mechanism is
the nonlinearity of the Sugawara construction.  Free becomes
interacting, finite tower becomes infinite tower.  The transition
is not continuous: $S_4 = 0$ for free fields,
$S_4 = 10/[c(5c{+}22)]$ for Virasoro.  No intermediate value.
The quartic invariant is either absent or nonzero; the tower
either terminates or runs forever.


% =================================================================
\section{The decisive test}
\label{sec:decisive-test}
% =================================================================

$F_2(V_{E_8}) = 7/1440$.

The bar complex says: $\kappa(E_8) = d/2 = 4$,
$F_2 = 4 \cdot 7/5760 = 7/1440$.

The Siegel--Weil formula says:
$\Theta_{E_8}^{(2)} = E_4^{(2)}$, the genus-$2$ Siegel
Eisenstein series of weight~$4$.  The Fourier coefficient
$a(\mathrm{diag}(1,1)) = 30240 = 240 \cdot 126$.
Each of the $240$ roots of $E_8$ has exactly $126$ orthogonal
roots.

These agree.  The machine works.

\sep

The agreement is a theorem: for any unimodular lattice $L$
of rank~$d$,
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lattice-f2}
F_2(V_L) \;=\; d \cdot \frac{7}{5760},
\end{equation}
and this equals the second coefficient of
$\log\Theta_L^{(2)}$ at the cusp.  The proof: the shadow
tower of a lattice vertex algebra terminates (class~G, all
$S_r = 0$ for $r \geq 3$), so
$F_g = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g^{\mathrm{FP}}$ at all genera,
where $\lambda_g^{\mathrm{FP}}$ is the Faber--Pandharipande
coefficient of the $\hat{A}$-genus.  The lattice theta function
$\Theta_L^{(g)} = E_{d/2}^{(g)}$ by Siegel--Weil, and the
logarithmic derivative at the cusp extracts the same
Bernoulli coefficient.

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lccc@{}}
\textbf{Lattice}
  & $d$
  & $\kappa = d/2$
  & $F_2 = \kappa \cdot 7/5760$ \\
\hline
$\Z$ (Heisenberg) & $1$ & $1/2$ & $7/11520$ \\
$D_4$ & $4$ & $2$ & $7/2880$ \\
$E_8$ & $8$ & $4$ & $7/1440$ \\
$E_8 \oplus E_8$ & $16$ & $8$ & $7/720$ \\
Leech & $24$ & $12$ & $7/480$
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip

Every entry is independently verifiable: $\kappa$ from the
OPE ($\kappa = d/2$ for $d$ free bosons), $F_2$ from the
$\hat{A}$-genus ($7/5760$), and $\Theta_L^{(2)}$ from lattice
theta functions in the Siegel modular forms literature.  The
three computations use different mathematics.  They give the
same number.


% =================================================================
\section{The $\cN = 2$ surprise}
\label{sec:n2-surprise}
% =================================================================

The $\cN = 2$ superconformal algebra is the first algebra outside
the standard Lie-theoretic landscape where the shadow obstruction tower was
computed.  Three results were unexpected.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{(a) The anomaly ratio (\textsc{corrected}).}\enspace
The naive channel sum $\kappa = c/2 + c/3 + c/3 = 7c/6$ is
\textbf{wrong}: it uses Zamolodchikov metric eigenvalues, which
are not anomaly ratios.  The correct kappa comes from the
Kazama--Suzuki coset decomposition of the $\cN = 2$ SCA from
$\fsl(2)_k$:
\[
\kappa(\cN{=}2)
\;=\; \kappa(\fsl(2)_k) + \kappa(\text{fermion pair})
      - \kappa(U(1)_{\text{denom}})
\;=\; \frac{3(k+2)}{4} + \frac{1}{2} - \Bigl(\frac{k}{2}+1\Bigr)
\;=\; \frac{k+4}{4}.
\]
In terms of $c = 3k/(k+2)$: $\kappa = (6-c)/(2(3-c))$.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{(b) Additive complementarity (\textsc{corrected}).}\enspace
$c' = 6 - c$, so $c + c' = 6$ (additive, like Virasoro's
$c + c' = 26$).  The Feigin--Frenkel involution for $\fsl(2)$
(dual Coxeter number $h^\vee = 2$) acts as $k \mapsto -k - 4$,
giving
\[
c' \;=\; \frac{3(k+4)}{k+2},
\qquad
c + c' \;=\; \frac{3k}{k+2} + \frac{3(k+4)}{k+2}
\;=\; \frac{3(2k+4)}{k+2} \;=\; 6.
\]
The complementarity sum $\kappa + \kappa' = 1$ is constant.
The Koszul involution $c \mapsto 6-c$ has its fixed point at
$c = 3$; however, $\kappa(3) = (6-3)/(2(3-3))$ has a pole there
(the coset denominator vanishes at the free-field limit
$k \to \infty$).  Self-duality at $c = 3$ holds at the level
of the central charge involution, not at the level of~$\kappa$.

\medskip
\noindent\textbf{(c) The chiral ring obstruction.}\enspace
The chiral ring $R = \C[x]/(x^{K-1})$ of an $\cN = 2$ theory at
level $K$ is a truncated polynomial ring.  It is \emph{not}
Koszul: $\Ext^n_R(\C,\C) = \C$ for all $n \geq 0$ (a
$2$-periodic resolution).  Koszulness of the full $\cN = 2$
superconformal algebra does not descend to the chiral ring.

\begin{warning}\label{warn:n2-conflict}
The direct OPE channel sum ($\kappa = 7c/6$) used
Zamolodchikov metric eigenvalues in place of anomaly ratios,
and is wrong (AP1).  The Kazama--Suzuki coset computation
($\kappa = (6{-}c)/(2(3{-}c))$, $c' = 6 - c$,
$\kappa + \kappa' = 1$) is the correct one.  The naive
formula $c' = 9/c$ (from setting $\kappa' = c'/2$ and
solving $7c/6 + 7c'/6 = \text{const}$) is a consequence of
the wrong kappa and is likewise dismissed.
\end{warning}


% =================================================================
\section{What the tables contain}
\label{sec:tables}
% =================================================================

$580$ shadow coefficients.  $15$ algebras.  $30$ arities each.
Every entry an exact rational number.
\begin{align*}
S_{10}(\mathrm{Vir},\, c = 1/2)
  &= \frac{3795318931456}{5764801}. \\[6pt]
S_{15}(\mathrm{Vir},\, c = 13)
  &= -\frac{83470593873707008}{259042567755018935661111}. \\[6pt]
F_3(\mathrm{Vir},\, c = 1/2)
  &= \frac{31}{3870720}.
\end{align*}

These numbers encode how deeply the bar complex of a chiral
algebra sees moduli-space geometry.  They are Taylor coefficients
of a square root: algebraic of degree~$2$, controlled by three
invariants $(\kappa, \alpha, S_4)$, branching over two points in
the $t$-plane.

The denominator of $S_r$ at arity $r \geq 4$ is
$(\mathrm{const}) \cdot c^{r-3}(5c+22)^{\lfloor(r-2)/2\rfloor}$
(Theorem~\ref{thm:denom-pattern}).  No Kac determinant beyond
weight~$4$ appears.  The numerator is a polynomial in $c$ with
integer coefficients whose degree grows linearly in~$r$.  The
signs oscillate with period governed by
$\arg\bigl(\text{root of } Q_L\bigr)$: a beat frequency between
the OPE poles and the Koszul sign.

Every number is computable.  None existed before this work.


% =================================================================
\section{The arithmetic of the shadow obstruction tower}
\label{sec:arithmetic-shadow-tower}
% =================================================================

The shadow metric $Q_L(t) = 4\kappa^2 + 12\kappa\alpha\,t
+ (9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta)\,t^2$ is a quadratic polynomial.
Its square root $\sqrt{Q_L}$ generates the shadow obstruction tower.
Everything in \S\ref{sec:one-quadratic-form}--\S\ref{sec:tables}
follows from this single algebraic function of degree~$2$.

The structure of this function is the structure of a quadratic
extension.  Not by analogy.  The same objects appear: discriminant,
ring of integers, splitting of primes, fundamental unit, regulator,
Galois involution, Dedekind zeta function.  They appear because
the square root of a quadratic polynomial over a function field
IS a quadratic extension.

\sep

\noindent\textbf{The extension.}\enspace
Fix a primary line $L$ with data $(\kappa, \alpha, S_4)$ and
function field $F = \Q(c)(t)$.  The shadow metric
$Q_L \in F$ is a quadratic polynomial.  Define
\begin{equation}\label{eq:shadow-extension}
  K_L \;:=\; F(\sqrt{Q_L}) \;=\; \Q(c)(t)(\sqrt{Q_L}).
\end{equation}
This is a quadratic extension of~$F$.  Its Galois group is
$\mathrm{Gal}(K_L/F) \cong \Z/2$, generated by the involution
$\sigma\colon \sqrt{Q_L} \mapsto -\sqrt{Q_L}$.

The shadow generating function
$H(t) = t^2\sqrt{Q_L(t)} \in K_L$
is an element of the extension that does not lie in the base field.
The shadow obstruction tower $\{S_r\}_{r \geq 2}$ consists of the Taylor
coefficients of $\sqrt{Q_L}$, which are the ``integers'' of~$K_L$
in the sense made precise below.

The Galois involution $\sigma$ sends $\sqrt{Q_L}$ to $-\sqrt{Q_L}$
and therefore $H(t)$ to $-H(t)$.  This sign is the Koszul sign:
the monodromy $-1$ of the shadow connection $\nabla^{\mathrm{sh}}$
around a branch point is the nontrivial element of
$\mathrm{Gal}(K_L/F)$.

\begin{proposition}[Discriminant of the shadow extension]
\label{prop:shadow-discriminant}
The discriminant of the quadratic extension $K_L/F$ is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:shadow-field-discriminant}
  \mathrm{disc}(K_L/F)
  \;=\;
  -32\,\kappa^2\,\Delta,
\end{equation}
where $\Delta = 8\kappa S_4$ is the critical discriminant.
For Virasoro:
$\mathrm{disc}(K_L/F) = -320\,c^2/(5c{+}22)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
The discriminant of the quadratic $At^2 + Bt + C$
with $A = 9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta$, $B = 12\kappa\alpha$,
$C = 4\kappa^2$ is $B^2 - 4AC
= 144\kappa^2\alpha^2 - 16\kappa^2(9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta)
= -32\kappa^2\Delta$.
\end{proof}

\sep

\begin{proposition}[Splitting behaviour of the shadow discriminant]
\label{prop:shadow-splitting}
Let $L$ be a primary line with critical discriminant
$\Delta_L = 8\kappa_L S_{4,L}$.  The splitting type of~$L$ in
the shadow extension is determined by $\Delta_L$:
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\roman*)}]
\item \emph{Split} ($\Delta_L = 0$, $\kappa_L \neq 0$).
  The shadow metric $Q_L$ is a perfect square:
  $Q_L = (2\kappa + 3\alpha\,t)^2$.  The extension is trivial
  on~$L$; $\sqrt{Q_L} \in F$.  The shadow obstruction tower terminates
  after finitely many terms.
  \textbf{Classes G and L.}  Heisenberg
  ($\alpha = 0$: terminates at arity~$2$) and affine Kac--Moody
  ($\alpha \neq 0$: terminates at arity~$3$).

\item \emph{Inert} ($\Delta_L \neq 0$).
  The shadow metric $Q_L$ is irreducible over $\Q(c)$.
  The extension $K_L/F$ is genuine; $\sqrt{Q_L} \notin F$.
  The shadow obstruction tower is infinite and the Taylor coefficients
  $S_r$ grow as $A\,\rho^r\,r^{-5/2}\cos(r\theta + \varphi)$.
  \textbf{Class M.}  Virasoro and $\cW_N$.

\item \emph{Ramified} ($\kappa_L = 0$).
  The shadow metric degenerates: $Q_L(t) = (9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta)\,t^2$,
  with a double zero at $t = 0$.  The curvature
  vanishes, the connection has an irregular singularity at the
  origin, and the shadow obstruction tower reduces to a monomial.
  This occurs on charged strata where the weight-changing
  line has $\kappa = 0$.  \textbf{Class C}
  (stratum separation: the quartic invariant lives on a
  stratum whose self-bracket exits the complex).
\end{enumerate}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
(i)~If $\Delta = 0$ then $S_4 = 0$ (since $\kappa \neq 0$),
so the coefficient of $t^2$ in $Q_L$ is $9\alpha^2$, and
$Q_L = (2\kappa + 3\alpha\,t)^2$.  The recursion
$S_r = -(1/(2r\kappa))\sum \varepsilon(j,k)\,jk\,S_j S_k$
yields $S_r = 0$ for all $r \geq 4$ when $S_4 = 0$
(each subsequent term vanishes by induction).
If also $\alpha = 0$: $S_3 = 0$ and the tower
terminates at $r = 2$.

(ii)~If $\Delta \neq 0$ then $\mathrm{disc}(Q_L)
= -32\kappa^2\Delta \neq 0$, so $Q_L$ has two distinct roots
$t_\pm \in \overline{F} \setminus F$, and $\sqrt{Q_L}$ is
algebraic of degree~$2$ over~$F$.
The Taylor coefficients of an algebraic function with
branch points at $t_\pm$ grow as
$|t_\pm|^{-r}\,r^{-3/2+\epsilon}$ by the Darboux transfer
theorem; the precise asymptotics
$A\,\rho^r\,r^{-5/2}\cos(r\theta + \varphi)$
follow from the exponent $-1/2$ of the branch-point singularity
$(t - t_\pm)^{1/2}$, giving $r^{-1-1/2} = r^{-3/2}$, combined
with the additional $r^{-1}$ from the Taylor extraction
$S_r = [t^{r-2}]\sqrt{Q_L}/(r)$.

(iii)~If $\kappa = 0$ then the constant and linear terms of
$Q_L$ vanish, and $Q_L(t) = (9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta)\,t^2$ has
a double zero at $t = 0$.  The flat section
$\Phi(t) = \sqrt{Q_L(t)/Q_L(0)}$ is undefined (the
connection has an irregular point at the origin).
\end{proof}

\sep

The four-class partition G/L/C/M is the splitting table of the
shadow quadratic extension.  Gauss's genus theory for binary
quadratic forms partitions forms of discriminant~$D$ into genera
by the values of the Legendre symbol $(D/p)$ at primes $p \mid D$.
Here, each primary line is a ``prime,'' and the critical
discriminant $\Delta_L$ plays the role of the Legendre symbol:
$\Delta_L = 0$ is split, $\Delta_L \neq 0$ is inert.

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{@{}llcll@{}}
\textbf{Class}
  & \textbf{Splitting}
  & $\Delta_L$
  & \textbf{$K_L/F$}
  & \textbf{Number field analogue}\\
\hline
G & totally split & $0$ ($\alpha = 0$)
  & trivial & $\Q(\sqrt{1}) = \Q$ \\
L & split & $0$ ($\alpha \neq 0$)
  & trivial & $\Q(\sqrt{d^2}) = \Q$ \\
C & ramified & $\kappa = 0$
  & degenerate & ramified prime \\
M & inert & $\neq 0$
  & genuine degree-$2$ & $\Q(\sqrt{d})$, $d < 0$ squarefree
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\sep

\noindent\textbf{The ring of integers.}\enspace
In $\Q(\sqrt{d})$, the ring of integers is $\Z[\omega]$ where
$\omega = \sqrt{d}$ or $(1+\sqrt{d})/2$ depending on
$d \bmod 4$.  In the shadow extension $K_L$, the ring of
integers is the ring of shadow coefficients:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:shadow-integers}
  \cO_{K_L}
  \;:=\;
  \Z[\kappa, \alpha, S_4]\bigl[\bigl\{S_r\bigr\}_{r \geq 5}\bigr]
  \;\subset\; K_L.
\end{equation}
The shadow coefficients $S_r$ for $r \geq 5$ are algebraic
integers over the base ring $\Z[\kappa, \alpha, S_4]$ because
they satisfy the recursion~\eqref{eq:recursive-road}
(the leading coefficient $1/(2r\kappa)$ is a unit in
$\Q(\kappa)$).

For Virasoro: $S_r \in \Q(c)$ for all $r \geq 2$, and the
denominator pattern (Theorem~\ref{thm:denom-pattern}) gives
\[
  \mathrm{denom}(S_r)
  \;=\;
  (\mathrm{const})\cdot
  c^{r-3}(5c{+}22)^{\lfloor(r-2)/2\rfloor}.
\]
Two primes appear: $c = 0$ and $c = -22/5$.  These are the
\emph{ramified primes} of the Virasoro shadow obstruction tower: the
places where the shadow coefficient has a pole.  They are the
zeros of the weight-$4$ Gram determinant
$\det G_4 = c^2(5c{+}22)/2$.  The shadow obstruction tower's poles are
the representation theory's zeros.

No Kac determinant beyond weight~$4$ appears.  The quadratic
extension sees only the first nontrivial Gram matrix.

\sep

\noindent\textbf{The growth rate analogue.}\enspace
In a real quadratic field $\Q(\sqrt{d})$, the fundamental unit
$\varepsilon = x_1 + y_1\sqrt{d}$ generates all units via
$\varepsilon^n$.  The regulator $R = \log|\varepsilon|$ measures
the size of the unit group.  (The specialised shadow fields
$K(c_0) = \Q(\sqrt{d(c_0)})$ are \emph{imaginary} quadratic,
so their unit group is $\{\pm 1\}$; the analogy below
concerns the branch-point growth rate, not literal units.)

In the shadow obstruction tower: the growth rate
\begin{equation}\label{eq:growth-rate-unit}
  \rho
  \;=\;
  \frac{\sqrt{9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta}}{2|\kappa|}
  \;=\;
  |t_+|^{-1},
\end{equation}
where $t_+$ is the zero of $Q_L$ nearest the origin, plays
the role of the fundamental unit.  The shadow ``regulator''
is $\log\rho$, measuring the exponential growth of the tower.

The convergence condition $\rho < 1$ means $|t_+| > 1$: the
branch points of $\sqrt{Q_L}$ lie outside the unit disk.  The
Taylor series $\sqrt{Q_L(t)} = \sum a_n t^n$ converges for
$|t| < |t_+|$, and the shadow obstruction tower converges as a formal power
series in the arity parameter.  Setting $\rho = 1$ yields the
critical cubic
\[
  5c^3 + 22c^2 - 180c - 872 \;=\; 0,
  \qquad c^* \approx 6.125,
\]
the boundary between convergent and divergent shadow obstruction towers:
the analogue of the Minkowski bound that controls the class
number of a number field.

For Virasoro at $c = 13$ (the self-dual point):
$\rho(13) \approx 0.467 < 1$.  The shadow obstruction tower converges
at the self-dual point.

\sep

\noindent\textbf{The Galois involution and Koszul duality.}\enspace
The Galois involution of $K_L/F$ is
$\sigma\colon \sqrt{Q_L} \mapsto -\sqrt{Q_L}$.
Koszul duality acts on the shadow extension by
$\cA \mapsto \cA^!$: for Virasoro, $c \mapsto 26 - c$.
These are different involutions that commute.

The Galois involution $\sigma$ is the monodromy of
$\nabla^{\mathrm{sh}}$: transport around a branch point
of $\sqrt{Q_L}$ returns $-\sqrt{Q_L}$.  This is the
Koszul sign of bar-cobar duality: the desuspension
$s^{-1}$ contributes a sign at each bar element.

The Koszul involution $\cA \mapsto \cA^!$ acts on the
coefficients of $Q_L$, not on $\sqrt{Q_L}$ itself.  For
Virasoro it sends $c \to 26 - c$, so
$\kappa \to (26-c)/2$, $\alpha \to \alpha'$,
$S_4 \to S_4'$.  The complementarity identity
\begin{equation}\label{eq:disc-complementarity-wn}
  \Delta(\cA) + \Delta(\cA^!)
  \;=\;
  \frac{6960}{(5c{+}22)(152{-}5c)}
\end{equation}
is the constraint that relates the discriminant of the
extension $K_L$ to the discriminant of the Koszul dual
extension~$K_{L!}$.  The numerator $6960 = 2^4 \cdot 3
\cdot 5 \cdot 29$ is universal.

At $c = 13$: both factors in the denominator equal $87$,
$\Delta(13) = 40/87$, and $\Delta + \Delta'
= 80/87 = 6960/87^2$.  The self-dual point is where the
two extensions $K_L$ and $K_{L!}$ coincide.

\sep

\noindent\textbf{The complementarity sum as class invariant.}\enspace
The complementarity sum
$\kappa(\cA) + \kappa(\cA^!)$ measures how far the Koszul
pair $(\cA, \cA^!)$ deviates from perfect anti-symmetry.

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lcc@{}}
\textbf{Family}
  & $\kappa + \kappa'$
  & \textbf{Status} \\
\hline
Kac--Moody & $0$ & anti-symmetric (FF involution around $0$) \\
Free fields & $0$ & anti-symmetric \\
Virasoro & $13$ & anti-symmetric around $c = 13$ \\
$\cN = 2$ & $1$ & anti-symmetric around $c = 3$
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip

In number theory, the class number $h(d)$ of $\Q(\sqrt{d})$
measures the failure of unique factorisation.  The
complementarity sum plays the analogous role for the Koszul
pair: it measures the failure of perfect duality.  When
$\kappa + \kappa' = 0$ (as for Kac--Moody), the pair is
``principal'' (the analogue of class number~$1$).  When
$\kappa + \kappa' \neq 0$ (as for Virasoro), the pair has
a nontrivial ``class invariant.''

\sep

\noindent\textbf{The Dirichlet series.}\enspace
For a number field~$K$, the Dedekind zeta function
$\zeta_K(s) = \sum_{\mathfrak{a}} N(\mathfrak{a})^{-s}$
encodes the arithmetic.  For a quadratic field:
$\zeta_{\Q(\sqrt{d})}(s) = \zeta(s) \cdot L(s, \chi_d)$
where $\chi_d$ is the Kronecker character.

In the shadow obstruction tower, the Dirichlet-sewing lift $S_\cA(u)$
of \S\ref{sec:prime-factorisation} plays this role:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Heisenberg:
  $S_\cH(u) = \zeta(u) \cdot \zeta(u{+}1)$.
  Two Riemann zeta functions: the simplest Dedekind zeta of
  a ``shadow quadratic field.''  Full Euler product.
\item Virasoro:
  $S_{\mathrm{Vir}}(u) = \zeta(u{+}1)\bigl(\zeta(u) - 1\bigr)$.
  The defect $(\zeta(u) - 1)$ breaks the Euler product.  The
  ratio $S_{\mathrm{Vir}}/S_\cH$ has singularities at the
  nontrivial zeros of $\zeta$: arithmetic data that the
  Heisenberg number field does not carry.
\end{itemize}

The Heisenberg sewing has no shadow obstruction tower (class~G,
$\Delta = 0$): its number field is $\Q$ itself, and the
Dirichlet series factors completely.  The Virasoro sewing has
an infinite shadow obstruction tower (class~M, $\Delta \neq 0$): its
number field is a genuine quadratic extension, and the Euler
product fails.

\sep

\noindent\textbf{The functional equation.}\enspace
The Dedekind zeta satisfies the functional equation
$\zeta_K(s) = |d_K|^{1/2-s}\,\gamma(s)\,\zeta_K(1{-}s)$,
where $d_K$ is the discriminant.  The shadow obstruction tower satisfies
a complementarity identity
\eqref{eq:disc-complementarity-wn} that plays the same
structural role: it relates the discriminant of $\cA$ to
the discriminant of $\cA^!$, with a universal numerator
independent of the family.

The complementarity identity is not a functional equation in
the classical sense (the shadow obstruction tower does not satisfy a
self-referential relation under $s \to 1-s$).  It is the
Koszul-dual constraint: the quadratic extension determined
by $\cA$ and the quadratic extension determined by $\cA^!$
are related by a universal formula.  The constant $6960$ is
the analogue of the conductor.

\sep

\noindent\textbf{What this is.}\enspace
The shadow obstruction tower is not a sequence of independent invariants.
It is not an analogy with number theory.  It is the Taylor
expansion of $\sqrt{Q_L}$ at the origin, and $\sqrt{Q_L}$
defines a quadratic extension $K_L = \Q(c)(t)(\sqrt{Q_L})$
of the function field $F = \Q(c)(t)$.  The entire tower is
the arithmetic of this extension: the discriminant is
$-32\kappa^2\Delta$; the splitting table is the G/L/C/M
classification; the growth rate is the reciprocal of the
nearest branch point; the Koszul sign is the Galois involution;
the complementarity identity is the duality constraint on
discriminants.

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{@{}ll@{}}
\textbf{Number field} & \textbf{Shadow obstruction tower} \\
\hline
$\Q(\sqrt{d})$ & $\Q(c)(t)(\sqrt{Q_L})$ \\[3pt]
Discriminant $d$ & $-32\kappa^2\Delta$ \\[3pt]
Ring of integers $\Z[\omega]$ & $\Z[\kappa,\alpha,S_4][\{S_r\}]$ \\[3pt]
Splitting of primes & G/L/C/M classification \\[3pt]
Fundamental unit $\varepsilon$ & Growth rate $\rho = |t_+|^{-1}$ \\[3pt]
Regulator $\log|\varepsilon|$ & $\log\rho$ \\[3pt]
Minkowski bound & Critical cubic $c^*$ \\[3pt]
Galois involution & Koszul sign (monodromy $-1$) \\[3pt]
Conductor & $6960$ \\[3pt]
Dedekind zeta $\zeta_K$ & Dirichlet-sewing lift $S_\cA(u)$ \\[3pt]
Class number & Complementarity sum $\kappa + \kappa'$ \\[3pt]
Genus theory & Four depth classes
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\medskip

The left column is realised over $\Q$.  The right column is
realised over $\Q(c)(t)$.  The same theorems hold because the
proofs depend only on the structure of a quadratic extension,
not on the coefficient field.


% =================================================================
\section{The specialisation functor}
\label{sec:specialisation-functor}
% =================================================================

The shadow extension $K_L = \Q(c)(t)(\sqrt{Q_L})$ of
\S\ref{sec:arithmetic-shadow-tower} lives over the function
field $\Q(c)$.  It is a generic quadratic extension: the
generic fibre of a family parametrised by the central charge.
The closed fibres are obtained by specialising $c$ to rational
values.

For each $c_0 \in \Q$ with $c_0 \neq 0$ and
$5c_0 + 22 \neq 0$, the \emph{specialised shadow obstruction tower} is
the sequence $S_r(c_0) = (1/r)[t^{r-2}]\sqrt{Q_L(t)}|_{c=c_0}$,
which consists of specific rational numbers.  The critical
discriminant specialises to
\begin{equation}\label{eq:delta-specialised}
  \Delta(c_0) \;=\; \frac{40}{5c_0 + 22} \;\in\; \Q,
\end{equation}
The discriminant of the shadow metric
$Q_L(t) = (9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta)\,t^2 + 12\kappa\alpha\,t + 4\kappa^2$
as a polynomial in~$t$ is
$\operatorname{disc}(Q_L) = -32\kappa^2\Delta < 0$
(for $\kappa, \Delta > 0$), so the zeros of $Q_L$ are complex
and $\Q(\sqrt{\operatorname{disc}(Q_L)})$ is an
\emph{imaginary} quadratic number field.

\begin{definition}[Specialised shadow field]
\label{def:specialised-shadow-field}
For $c_0 \in \Q$ with $c_0(5c_0 + 22) \neq 0$, the
\emph{specialised shadow field} is
\begin{equation}\label{eq:specialised-field}
  K(c_0) \;:=\; \Q\bigl(\sqrt{-2\Delta(c_0)}\bigr)
  \;=\; \Q\!\left(\sqrt{d(c_0)}\right)
\end{equation}
where $d(c_0) < 0$ is the squarefree part of $-2\Delta(c_0)$,
computed as follows.  Write $5c_0 + 22 = a/b$ in lowest terms.
Then $-2\Delta(c_0) = -80b/a$, and
$K(c_0) = \Q(\sqrt{-80ab}) = \Q(\sqrt{\mathrm{sqfree}(-80ab)})$.
Since $-80 = -2^4 \cdot 5$, the squarefree part is
$-5\cdot\mathrm{sqfree}(ab)$ when $\gcd(\mathrm{sqfree}(ab), 5) = 1$,
with appropriate adjustment otherwise.
\end{definition}

\sep

\noindent\textbf{Computation.}\enspace
The minimal model central charges are
$c_{p,q} = 1 - 6(p-q)^2/(pq)$ for coprime integers
$2 \leq p < q$.  Each gives a specific quadratic field.

\medskip

\noindent\textit{$c = 1/2$ (Ising model, $M(3,4)$).}\enspace
$5c + 22 = 49/2$, so $a = 49$, $b = 2$.
$-80ab = -80 \cdot 49 \cdot 2 = -7840 = -2^5 \cdot 5 \cdot 7^2$.
$\mathrm{sqfree}(-7840) = -10$.
$K(1/2) = \Q(\sqrt{-10})$.
Discriminant~$-40$ (since $-10 \equiv 2 \pmod{4}$,
disc $= 4 \cdot (-10)$).  Class number $h = 2$.

The shadow obstruction tower of the Ising model lives over an imaginary
quadratic field.

\medskip

\noindent\textit{$c = 7/10$ (tricritical Ising, $M(4,5)$).}\enspace
$5c + 22 = 51/2$, so $a = 51$, $b = 2$.
$-80ab = -80 \cdot 51 \cdot 2 = -8160 = -2^5 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 \cdot 17$.
$\mathrm{sqfree}(-8160) = -2 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 \cdot 17 = -510$.
$K(7/10) = \Q(\sqrt{-510})$.
Discriminant $4 \cdot (-510) = -2040$.
Class number $h = 4$.

\medskip

\noindent\textit{$c = 4/5$ (three-state Potts, $M(5,6)$).}\enspace
$5c + 22 = 26$, so $a = 26$, $b = 1$.
$-80ab = -80 \cdot 26 = -2080 = -2^5 \cdot 5 \cdot 13$.
$\mathrm{sqfree}(-2080) = -2 \cdot 5 \cdot 13 = -130$.
$K(4/5) = \Q(\sqrt{-130})$.
Discriminant $4 \cdot (-130) = -520$.
Class number $h = 4$.

\medskip

\noindent\textit{$c = 1$ (free boson compactified).}\enspace
$5c + 22 = 27 = 3^3$, so $a = 27$, $b = 1$.
$-80ab = -80 \cdot 27 = -2160 = -2^4 \cdot 3^3 \cdot 5$.
$\mathrm{sqfree}(-2160) = -3 \cdot 5 = -15$.
$K(1) = \Q(\sqrt{-15})$.
Discriminant~$-15$ (since $-15 \equiv 1 \pmod{4}$).
Class number $h = 2$.

\medskip

\noindent\textit{$c = 13$ (self-dual point).}\enspace
$5c + 22 = 87 = 3 \cdot 29$, so $a = 87$, $b = 1$.
$-80ab = -80 \cdot 87 = -6960 = -2^4 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 \cdot 29$.
% RECTIFICATION-FLAG: sqfree(-6960) = -435 = -(3)(5)(29), not -870.
% Since -6960 = -(2^4)(3)(5)(29) = -(2^2)^2 (3)(5)(29).
$\mathrm{sqfree}(-6960) = -3 \cdot 5 \cdot 29 = -435$.
$K(13) = \Q(\sqrt{-435})$.
Discriminant~$-435$ (since $-435 \equiv 1 \pmod{4}$).
Class number $h = 7$.

The complementarity constant
$6960 = 2^4 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 \cdot 29$ appears as
$-80 \cdot 87 = -6960$, so the self-dual shadow field is
$\Q(\sqrt{-435})$ where $435 = 6960/16$.

\medskip

\noindent\textit{$c = 25$ (Koszul dual of $c = 1$).}\enspace
$5c + 22 = 147 = 3 \cdot 7^2$, so $a = 147$, $b = 1$.
$-80ab = -80 \cdot 147 = -11760 = -2^4 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 \cdot 7^2$.
$\mathrm{sqfree}(-11760) = -3 \cdot 5 = -15$.
$K(25) = \Q(\sqrt{-15})$.

\textbf{The same field as $c = 1$.}

\medskip

\noindent\textit{$c = 26$ (critical string).}\enspace
$5c + 22 = 152 = 2^3 \cdot 19$, so $a = 152$, $b = 1$.
$-80ab = -80 \cdot 152 = -12160 = -2^7 \cdot 5 \cdot 19$.
$\mathrm{sqfree}(-12160) = -2 \cdot 5 \cdot 19 = -190$.
$K(26) = \Q(\sqrt{-190})$.
Discriminant $4 \cdot (-190) = -760$.
Class number $h = 4$.

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lccrcr@{}}
$c$
  & $\Delta(c)$
  & $d(c)$
  & $\mathrm{disc}$
  & $h$
  & $\rho(c)$ \\
\hline
$1/2$ & $80/49$ & $-10$ & $-40$ & $2$
  & $12.53$ \\[2pt]
$7/10$ & $80/51$ & $-510$ & $-2040$ & $4$
  & $8.94$ \\[2pt]
$4/5$ & $20/13$ & $-130$ & $-520$ & $4$
  & $7.81$ \\[2pt]
$1$ & $40/27$ & $-15$ & $-15$ & $2$
  & $6.24$ \\[2pt]
$13$ & $40/87$ & $-435$ & $-435$ & $7$
  & $0.47$ \\[2pt]
$25$ & $40/147$ & $-15$ & $-15$ & $2$
  & $0.24$ \\[2pt]
$26$ & $5/19$ & $-190$ & $-760$ & $4$
  & $0.23$
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\noindent
The fields are \emph{imaginary} quadratic: the zeros of
$Q_L(t)$ are complex ($\operatorname{disc}(Q_L) < 0$).
The unit group is $\{\pm 1\}$ (no fundamental unit).

\sep

\begin{proposition}[Koszul field-preservation criterion]
\label{prop:koszul-field-criterion}
The Koszul pair $(\mathrm{Vir}_c,\, \mathrm{Vir}_{26-c})$
gives the same specialised shadow field
$K(c) = K(26-c)$
if and only if the ratio
\begin{equation}\label{eq:koszul-field-ratio}
  \frac{5c + 22}{152 - 5c}
  \quad\in\quad \Q^{\times 2}
\end{equation}
is a perfect square in $\Q^\times$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
$K(c) = \Q(\sqrt{-80(5c{+}22)})$ and
$K(26{-}c) = \Q(\sqrt{-80(152{-}5c)})$
(up to squarefree extraction from the denominator, absorbed
by clearing denominators).  Two quadratic fields
$\Q(\sqrt{a})$ and $\Q(\sqrt{b})$ coincide iff $a/b$ is a
perfect square.  The factor $-80$ cancels in the ratio.
\end{proof}

\begin{example}[The pair $c = 1$, $c = 25$]
$(5 + 22)/(152 - 5) = 27/147 = 9/49 = (3/7)^2$.
Both give $K = \Q(\sqrt{-15})$.
\end{example}

\begin{example}[The self-dual point $c = 13$]
$(65 + 22)/(152 - 65) = 87/87 = 1 = 1^2$.
Trivially the same field $K = \Q(\sqrt{-435})$.
\end{example}

\begin{remark}[Failure for minimal models]
\label{rem:koszul-field-failure}
For the Ising model $c = 1/2$, the Koszul dual is
$c' = 51/2$.  The ratio $(49/2)/(299/2) = 49/299$,
and $299 = 13 \cdot 23$ is squarefree, so $49/299$
is not a perfect square.  The fields are different:
$K(1/2) = \Q(\sqrt{-10})$ while
$K(51/2) = \Q(\sqrt{-1495})$ where
$1495 = 5 \cdot 13 \cdot 23$.

More generally: for every minimal model central charge
$c_{p,q}$ with $p < 10$, the Koszul pair gives
different imaginary quadratic fields.  The criterion
\eqref{eq:koszul-field-ratio} requires the specific
arithmetic coincidence $\mathrm{sqfree}(5c+22) =
\mathrm{sqfree}(152-5c)$, which fails generically.

Among integers $c \in [0, 26]$, it holds only at
$c = 1$ and $c = 13$.
\end{remark}

\sep

\begin{proposition}[Complementarity constrains the field pair]
\label{prop:field-complementarity}
For any $c_0 \in \Q$ with $c_0(5c_0{+}22)(152{-}5c_0) \neq 0$,
the discriminants of the Koszul pair satisfy
\begin{equation}\label{eq:delta-product}
  \Delta(c_0) \cdot \Delta(26 - c_0)
  \;=\;
  \frac{1600}{(5c_0 + 22)(152 - 5c_0)},
\end{equation}
and the complementarity identity
\begin{equation}\label{eq:delta-sum-specialised}
  \Delta(c_0) + \Delta(26 - c_0)
  \;=\;
  \frac{6960}{(5c_0 + 22)(152 - 5c_0)}.
\end{equation}
The numerators $1600 = 40^2$ and $6960 = 2^4 \cdot 3 \cdot 5
\cdot 29$ are independent of $c_0$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Direct computation:
$\Delta(c)\,\Delta(26{-}c) = 40/(5c{+}22) \cdot 40/(152{-}5c)$.
The sum is $40(152{-}5c + 5c{+}22)/((5c{+}22)(152{-}5c))
= 40 \cdot 174/((5c{+}22)(152{-}5c)) = 6960/((5c{+}22)(152{-}5c))$.
\end{proof}

The pair $(K(c),\, K(26{-}c))$ is not determined by the
complementarity identity alone, but the identity constrains it:
the two discriminants are roots of the quadratic
$x^2 - (6960/P)\,x + 1600/P = 0$
where $P = (5c+22)(152-5c)$.

\sep

\noindent\textbf{What fails.}\enspace
The shadow growth rate $\rho(c) = \sqrt{(180c + 872)/(5c+22)}\,/\,c$
is a smooth function of~$c$.  The class number
$h(d(c))$ of the specialised shadow field $K(c)$ is a
function of the squarefree part $d(c) < 0$, which depends on $c$
through the prime factorisation of $(5c+22)_{\mathrm{num}}
\cdot (5c+22)_{\mathrm{den}}$.  This is a wildly discontinuous
function of~$c$: a small change in $c$ can change the squarefree
part arbitrarily.

\begin{observation}[No relation between $\rho$ and $h$]
\label{obs:no-rho-epsilon}
The growth rate $\rho$ and the class number $h$
arise from different constructions applied to the same input.
$\rho$ comes from the branch point of $\sqrt{Q_L}$ (OPE
data).  $h$ comes from the ideal class group of $\Q(\sqrt{d(c)})$
(prime factorisation data).

At $c = 1/2$: $\rho \approx 12.53$,
$h = 2$.
At $c = 13$: $\rho \approx 0.467$,
$h = 7$.

No algebraic relation connects them.
The proof: $\rho(c)$ is a smooth algebraic function of~$c$,
while $h(d(c))$ is a step function with
discontinuities at every $c$ where the squarefree part
of $-80(5c+22)$ changes.  A smooth function cannot
equal a step function.
\end{observation}

\sep

\noindent\textbf{What the specialisation sees.}\enspace
The specialisation functor $c \mapsto K(c)$ sends the
generic shadow extension $K_L/\Q(c)(t)$ to a specific
quadratic number field for each rational $c$.  This
functor is:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item Not injective: $c = 1$ and $c = 25$ give the same
  field $\Q(\sqrt{-15})$.
\item Not continuous: nearby rational values of $c$ can
  give completely different fields (different squarefree
  parts, different class numbers).
\item Koszul-equivariant only at special points: the
  Koszul involution $c \mapsto 26-c$ preserves the field
  iff $(5c+22)/(152-5c) \in \Q^{\times 2}$, which holds
  for $c = 1, 13$ and fails generically.
\end{enumerate}

The family of quadratic fields $\{K(c_{p,q})\}$ attached to
the minimal model tower is a new arithmetic invariant of 2d
CFT.  Its structure is controlled by the prime factorisation
of $5c_{p,q} + 22$.

\begin{conjecture}[Class number growth along the minimal model tower]
\label{conj:class-number-growth}
The class number $h(d(c_{p,q}))$ grows without bound as
$p + q \to \infty$ along the minimal model tower.
Equivalently: the squarefree parts $d(c_{p,q})$ produce
quadratic fields of increasing arithmetic complexity.
\end{conjecture}

This is a number-theoretic conjecture about the factorisation
of the linear forms $5c_{p,q} + 22$, analogous to the
classical conjecture on class numbers of imaginary quadratic
fields $\Q(\sqrt{-p})$ for primes~$p$.  The shadow obstruction tower reduces the question to the
behaviour of the denominator polynomial
$5c + 22$ under prime factorisation as $c$ ranges over
the minimal model spectrum.


% =================================================================
\section{The descent chain}
\label{sec:descent-chain}
% =================================================================

The preceding sections (\S\ref{sec:fourier}--\S\ref{sec:specialisation-functor})
construct, one level at a time, a chain of identifications between
structures in operadic algebra and structures in analytic number
theory.  This section subjects the chain to the Beilinson test:
which links are theorems, which are structural analogies, and
which are false.

\subsection{The five levels}

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}
\begin{tabular}{@{}clll@{}}
\textbf{Level} & \textbf{Object} & \textbf{Involution} & \textbf{Status} \\
\hline
0 & Operadic Koszul duality
  & $\cA \mapsto \cA^!$
  & Theorem (A) \\[3pt]
1 & Bar construction as Fourier kernel
  & $\B_X(\cA) \xrightarrow{\bD_{\Ran}} \B_X(\cA^!)$
  & Theorem (A) \\[3pt]
2 & Shadow obstruction tower as quadratic extension
  & $\sigma\colon \sqrt{Q_L} \mapsto -\sqrt{Q_L}$
  & Proved (\S\ref{sec:arithmetic-shadow-tower}) \\[3pt]
3 & Dirichlet-sewing lift $S_\cA(u)$
  & Koszul $\cA \mapsto \cA^!$ on weights
  & Partially defined (\S\ref{sec:prime-factorisation}) \\[3pt]
4 & Zeros of $\varepsilon^c_s$ on critical lines
  & $s \mapsto c/2 + 1 - s$
  & Proved for lattice VOAs
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The claim under examination: these five levels are connected by
a single descent, and the involution at each level is the shadow
of a single Fourier duality.

\subsection{Level 0 to Level 1: the bar construction as Fourier transform}

\begin{proposition}[Bar = categorical Fourier]
\label{prop:bar-fourier}
The bar construction $\B_X(\cA)$ satisfies:
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\alph*)}]
\item $\bD_{\Ran}(\B_X(\cA)) \simeq \B_X(\cA^!)$
  \textup{(}Theorem~A: convolution to pointwise\textup{)}.
\item $\Omega_X(\B_X(\cA)) \xrightarrow{\sim} \cA$
  \textup{(}Theorem~B: inversion on the Koszul locus\textup{)}.
\item $Q_g(\cA) \oplus Q_g(\cA^!) \cong H^*(\overline\cM_g,\cZ_\cA)$
  \textup{(}Theorem~C: Plancherel\textup{)}.
\end{enumerate}
These three properties characterise the classical Fourier
transform: intertwining convolution with multiplication,
self-inversion, and Parseval's identity.
\end{proposition}

This is a theorem, not an analogy.  The bar construction is
literally a Fourier transform in the sense of a monoidal
involution on factorisation (co)algebras on $\Ran(X)$, with
Verdier duality playing the role of complex conjugation.

\begin{warning}[Scope of the identification]
\label{warn:bar-fourier-scope}
The identification ``bar = Fourier'' is precise at the categorical
level: $\B_X$ is a functor between $\infty$-categories of
factorisation algebras and coalgebras on $\Ran(X)$, with properties
(a)--(c) paralleling $\cF\colon L^2 \to L^2$.  It is \emph{not}
a Fourier transform in the $L^2$ sense: there is no Hilbert space,
no Plancherel measure, and no pointwise evaluation.  The kernel
$\eta_{ij} = d\log(z_i - z_j)$ is a section of
$\omega_{\overline{C}_n(X)}(\log D)$, not a character of an abelian
group.

The one case where the identification is literally classical:
Heisenberg on an elliptic curve, where $\B_X(\cH_k)$ is the
Fourier--Mukai kernel on $\Jac(E) \times \Jac(E)^\vee$.
For non-abelian algebras, the bar construction is the
non-abelian Fourier transform, as the Grothendieck simultaneous
resolution is the non-abelian Springer resolution.
\end{warning}

\textbf{Verdict on Level 0$\to$1}: PROVED.  The bar construction
satisfies the three defining properties of a Fourier transform
(intertwining, inversion, Plancherel) as theorems of the monograph
(Theorems A, B, C).  The kernel $d\log(z_i - z_j)$ is the
chiral analogue of $e^{-2\pi i x\xi}$.

\subsection{Level 1 to Level 2: from bar curvature to quadratic extension}

The bar curvature $\kappa(\cA) = \text{Res}_{s=1}\, F_1(\cA)$
seeds the shadow metric $Q_L(t) = 4\kappa^2 + 12\kappa\alpha\,t
+ (9\alpha^2 + 2\Delta)\,t^2$.  The shadow obstruction tower
$\{S_r\}_{r \geq 2}$ consists of the Taylor coefficients of
$\sqrt{Q_L(t)}$.  This defines a quadratic extension
$K_L = \Q(c)(t)(\sqrt{Q_L})$ of the function field $F = \Q(c)(t)$,
with:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Galois group $\Z/2$, generated by
  $\sigma\colon \sqrt{Q_L} \mapsto -\sqrt{Q_L}$,
\item discriminant $-32\kappa^2\Delta$,
\item splitting table = G/L/C/M classification.
\end{itemize}

\begin{proposition}[The Galois involution is the Koszul sign]
\label{prop:galois-koszul-sign}
The monodromy of the shadow connection
$\nabla^{\mathrm{sh}} = d - Q_L'/(2Q_L)\,dt$ around each branch
point of $\sqrt{Q_L}$ is $-1$.  This is the Koszul sign: the
desuspension $s^{-1}$ in the bar construction contributes $(-1)^n$
at bar degree~$n$.  The Galois involution of $K_L/F$ is the action
of $s^{-1}$ on the shadow generating function.
\end{proposition}

This is proved (the monodromy computation is direct).
But the identification of the Galois involution with the Koszul
sign is a \emph{consequence} of the algebraic structure, not
a descent of the Fourier involution.  More precisely:

\begin{warning}[Two involutions, not one]
\label{warn:two-involutions}
The bar-cobar Verdier duality (Level 1) sends $\cA$ to
$\cA^!$ by acting on $\Ran(X)$.  The Galois involution of the
shadow extension (Level 2) sends $\sqrt{Q_L}$ to $-\sqrt{Q_L}$
by acting on the arity variable~$t$.  These are different
involutions acting on different spaces.  The Koszul involution
$\cA \mapsto \cA^!$ acts on the \emph{coefficients} of $Q_L$
(sending $\kappa \to \kappa'$, $\alpha \to \alpha'$,
$S_4 \to S_4'$); it does \emph{not} negate $\sqrt{Q_L}$.

The complementarity identity
$\Delta(\cA) + \Delta(\cA^!) = 6960/[(5c{+}22)(152{-}5c)]$
relates the discriminants of the two extensions $K_L$ and
$K_{L!}$.  It is a constraint between $K_L$ and $K_{L!}$,
not a symmetry of $K_L$ alone.  At $c = 13$: $K_L = K_{L!}$,
and the Koszul involution preserves the extension; at generic
$c$: $K_L \neq K_{L!}$ (Remark~\ref{rem:koszul-field-failure}).

To summarise: the Galois involution ($\sigma$) is intrinsic
to a single algebra~$\cA$ and its shadow obstruction tower.  The Koszul
involution ($\cA \mapsto \cA^!$) relates two algebras and their
two shadow obstruction towers.  They coincide only at the self-dual point.
\end{warning}

\textbf{Verdict on Level 1$\to$2}: PROVED that the shadow
tower is a quadratic extension with Galois involution $= $ Koszul
sign.  FALSE that this Galois involution is the ``descent'' of
the Verdier duality from Level 1.  The Galois involution and the
Koszul involution are two distinct $\Z/2$-actions that commute
but do not coincide.

\subsection{Level 2 to Level 3: the Dirichlet-sewing lift}

The Dirichlet-sewing lift $S_\cA(u) = \zeta(u{+}1)\sum_i
(\zeta(u) - H_{w_i - 1}(u))$ is defined from the bosonic weight
multiset $W(\cA) = \{w_i\}$.  For Heisenberg ($W = \{1\}$):
$S_\cH(u) = \zeta(u)\zeta(u{+}1)$, with full Euler product.
For Virasoro ($W = \{2\}$):
$S_{\mathrm{Vir}}(u) = \zeta(u{+}1)(\zeta(u) - 1)$,
with broken Euler product.

\begin{warning}[The Dirichlet-sewing lift is not derived from the shadow obstruction tower]
\label{warn:dirichlet-not-shadow}
The function $S_\cA(u)$ is defined from the weight multiset
$W(\cA)$, which is genus-$0$ data (the conformal weights of
the generating fields).  The shadow obstruction tower $\{S_r\}$ is genus-$1$
and higher data (the MC element $\Theta_\cA$, projected to arities
$r \geq 2$).

These are logically independent.  The shadow obstruction tower is determined
by $(\kappa, \alpha, S_4)$; the Dirichlet-sewing lift is
determined by $W(\cA)$.  For the four-class landscape:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item Heisenberg: $\kappa = 1/2$, $\alpha = 0$,
  $S_4 = 0$, $W = \{1\}$, $S_\cH(u) = \zeta(u)\zeta(u{+}1)$.
\item Virasoro: $\kappa = c/2$, $\alpha = 2$,
  $S_4 = 10/[c(5c{+}22)]$, $W = \{2\}$,
  $S_{\mathrm{Vir}}(u) = \zeta(u{+}1)(\zeta(u) - 1)$.
\end{itemize}
The Dirichlet-sewing lift sees only the weight multiset; the
shadow obstruction tower sees the OPE structure constants.  They are
related only insofar as both derive from the same chiral
algebra, but neither determines the other.

In particular: two algebras with the same weight multiset
but different OPE coefficients have the same $S_\cA(u)$ but
different shadow obstruction towers.  Two algebras with the same
$(\kappa, \alpha, S_4)$ but different weight multiplicities
have the same shadow obstruction tower but different $S_\cA(u)$.
\end{warning}

\textbf{Verdict on Level 2$\to$3}: the link is BROKEN.  The
Dirichlet-sewing lift and the shadow obstruction tower are independent invariants
of the chiral algebra.  Neither descends from the other.  The Koszul
involution acts on $S_\cA(u)$ by replacing $W(\cA)$ with $W(\cA^!)$:
this is a well-defined operation, but it is not the ``descent'' of
the Galois involution of the shadow extension, which acts on
$\sqrt{Q_L}$ and has no knowledge of $W(\cA)$.

\subsection{Level 3 to Level 4: zeros and functional equations}

For lattice vertex algebras, the constrained Epstein zeta
$\varepsilon^c_s$ factors into $L$-functions via the Hecke
decomposition of the theta function
(Theorem~\ref{thm:shadow-spectral-correspondence}).  The
functional equation of $\varepsilon^c_s$ follows from the
modular invariance of~$Z$, with the symmetry
$s \mapsto c/2 + 1 - s$ determined by the weight.

\begin{warning}[Structural obstruction to a ``shadow Riemann hypothesis'']
\label{warn:no-shadow-rh}
The scattering matrix $\varphi(s) = \Lambda(1{-}s)/\Lambda(s)$ has
poles at complex spectral parameters $s = \rho/2$, where $\rho$
ranges over the nontrivial zeros of~$\zeta$.  The MC equation and
the shadow obstruction tower constrain the spectral coefficients on the
\emph{real} spectral axis $s = 1/2 + it$, $t \in \bR$.  The zeta
zeros live at \emph{complex}~$t$
(Remark~\ref{rem:structural-obstruction} in
\texttt{arithmetic\_shadows.tex}).

This separation is structural.  The shadow obstruction tower is determined by
finitely many OPE coefficients $(\kappa, \alpha, S_4)$; the
nontrivial zeros of $\zeta$ encode infinitely many independent
quantities.  No finite-dimensional algebraic structure can
determine the zeros of an entire function of order~$1$.  The
``shadow Riemann hypothesis'' (all zeros of $\varepsilon^c_s$ on
critical lines) is a consequence of GRH for the constituent
$L$-functions, not a consequence of the MC equation.

The Li coefficient test
(\texttt{compute/lib/dirichlet\_sewing.py}): the modified Li
coefficients $\tilde\lambda_n(\cH)$ for the Heisenberg sewing lift
$S_\cH(u) = \zeta(u)\zeta(u{+}1)$ are positive for
$n = 1, \ldots, 6$ and \textbf{negative at $n = 7$}.  Positivity
of all Li coefficients would be equivalent to GRH for
$\zeta(u)\zeta(u{+}1)$, which is equivalent to RH for $\zeta$
itself.  The sign change at $n = 7$ does not disprove RH (the Li
coefficients for $\zeta$ alone are positive; the sign change is an
artefact of the product $\zeta(u)\zeta(u{+}1)$ and the particular
regularisation).  But it does show that the sewing-lift Li
coefficients do not have the positivity properties needed for a
``shadow RH.''
\end{warning}

\textbf{Verdict on Level 3$\to$4}: PROVED for lattice VOAs (via
Hecke decomposition).  For non-lattice algebras: the
Dirichlet-sewing lift $S_\cA(u)$ is not a Dirichlet series with
multiplicative coefficients, so the $L$-function factorisation does
not apply.  No mechanism in the MC equation forces zeros onto
critical lines.

\subsection{The descent chain: what is proved and what fails}

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}
\begin{tabular}{@{}ccl@{}}
\textbf{Link} & \textbf{Status} & \textbf{Obstruction} \\
\hline
$0 \to 1$ & \textbf{Proved} & None.  Bar = categorical Fourier. \\[3pt]
$1 \to 2$ & \textbf{Proved}$^*$ &
  Two involutions, not one.
  $^*$The shadow obstruction tower is a quadratic extension;\\
  & & the Galois involution is the Koszul sign.
  But the Galois \\
  & & involution is \emph{not} the descent of Verdier duality. \\[3pt]
$2 \to 3$ & \textbf{Broken} &
  The Dirichlet-sewing lift and the shadow obstruction tower are\\
  & & independent invariants.  Neither descends from the other.\\[3pt]
$3 \to 4$ & \textbf{Lattice only} &
  Non-lattice algebras lack Euler products.\\
  & & Structural obstruction:
  OPE data $\not\Rightarrow$ zeta zeros.
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\subsection{The four Dirichlet series: a disambiguation that matters}
\label{sec:four-dirichlet-disambiguation}

The preceding analysis of the descent chain reveals that the
arithmetic programme involves not one but \emph{four} distinct
Dirichlet series, each measuring a different invariant of the
chiral algebra.  The failure to distinguish them has been the
source of every false claim about ``shadow $L$-functions'' and
``shadow Riemann hypotheses'' in the development of this chapter.

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}
\begin{tabular}{@{}cllll@{}}
& \textbf{Object} & \textbf{Input data} & \textbf{Axis}
  & \textbf{Arithmetic depth} \\
\hline
\textup{(a)} & Shadow-metric Epstein $\varepsilon_{Q_L}(s)$
  & $(\kappa, \alpha, \Delta)$
  & Arity (genus 0)
  & Quadratic field \\[3pt]
\textup{(b)} & Constrained Epstein $\varepsilon^c_s$
  & Primary spectrum $\{(\Delta_i, d_i)\}$
  & Genus (genus 1)
  & $L$-functions (lattice) \\[3pt]
\textup{(c)} & Shadow Dirichlet $\zeta_\cA(s)$
  & Shadow tower $\{S_r\}$
  & Arity (genus 0)
  & Entire, no Euler product \\[3pt]
\textup{(d)} & Categorical zeta $\zeta^{\mathrm{DK}}_\fg(s)$
  & $\{\dim V_\lambda\}$
  & Lie-theoretic
  & $\zeta(s)$ for $\fsl_2$
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\noindent Plus the Dirichlet-sewing lift
$S_\cA(u) = \zeta(u{+}1)\sum_i (\zeta(u) - H_{w_i - 1}(u))$,
built from the weight multiset $W(\cA)$---a fifth object
independent of both (a)--(c) and (d).

\textbf{The resolution of the Benjamin--Chang tension.}
Benjamin--Chang~\cite{BenjaminChang22} proved that the
nontrivial Riemann zeta zeros~$\rho_n$ appear as poles at
$s = (1 + \rho_n)/2$ in the spectral decomposition of the
constrained Epstein~(b) on $\mathrm{SL}(2,\Z)\backslash
\mathfrak{H}$, via the scattering factor
$\zeta(2s)/\zeta(2s{-}1)$ in the Eisenstein series~$E_s$.
This is a theorem about the spectral theory of the
\emph{moduli space}~$\cM_{1,1}$: the Riemann zeta zeros enter
because the Eisenstein series' functional equation is governed
by the Euler product of~$\zeta(s)$.

The ``negative results'' (shadow zeta $\notin$ Selberg class,
no Euler product, $c$-duality $\neq$ $s$-reflection)
concern object~(c), the shadow Dirichlet series.  There is no
tension: (b) and (c) are different Dirichlet series measuring
different invariants.  The shadow zeta~(c) lives on the arity axis
at genus~$0$; the constrained Epstein~(b) lives on the genus
axis at genus~$1$.  The Riemann zeta enters through the spectral
theory of~$\cM_{1,1}$ (the genus axis) and through the
Dirichlet-sewing lift (the weight multiset), not through the
shadow obstruction tower (the arity axis).

In the bigraded shadow algebra $\cA^{\mathrm{sh}}_{r,g}$:
the shadow obstruction tower is the $(r, 0)$ slice, the
constrained Epstein is extracted from the $(0, 1)$ slice, and
the genus tower $F_g = \kappa \lambda_g^{\mathrm{FP}}$ is the
$(0, g)$ slice.  The descent chain breaks at Level~$2 \to 3$
because it attempts to connect orthogonal axes of the bigraded
structure.  The correct picture is the \emph{fan}
(\S\ref{sec:fan-picture} below): three independent projections
from a single MC element, with the Riemann zeta entering at
the genus projection (via $\cM_{1,1}$) but absent from the
arity projection (the shadow obstruction tower).

\subsection{The positivity frontier: from genus $1$ to genus $2$}
\label{sec:positivity-frontier}

The integrability--positivity dichotomy is the root obstruction
to connecting the bar-cobar programme to the Riemann Hypothesis.
The MC equation $D\Theta + \tfrac{1}{2}[\Theta,\Theta] = 0$ is
\emph{integrability} (flatness); every spectral conclusion
(Ramanujan bounds, $L$-value non-vanishing, zero locations)
requires \emph{positivity} (sign-definiteness).  These are
logically independent.

At genus~$1$, three independent obstructions are proved:
(i)~unfolding erasure (the Rankin--Selberg integral is
holomorphic at all scattering poles),
(ii)~real--complex separation (MC constrains the real spectral
axis; zeta zeros live at complex parameters),
(iii)~dimensional underdetermination (three numbers
$\kappa, \alpha, S_4$ cannot determine infinitely many zeros).
All three are \textbf{genus-$1$ theorems}
(Theorem~\ref{thm:structural-separation}).

At genus~$\geq 2$, the structural obstruction is bypassed:
the B\"ocherer bridge
(Theorem~\ref{thm:bocherer-bridge})
provides algebraic access to central $L$-values
$L(\tfrac{1}{2}, \pi_f \times \chi_D)$, and the off-diagonal
sewing blocks $R_{12}, R_{21}$ break the Fock-diagonality that
enables genus-$1$ erasure.

\textbf{The surviving research programme: the genus-$2$ bar
Laplacian and $\mathrm{Sp}(4)$ Arthur packets.}

The genus-$2$ bar Laplacian
$\Delta_B^{(2)} = d_B^{(2)}(d_B^{(2)})^*
+ (d_B^{(2)})^* d_B^{(2)}$
inherits a $2 \times 2$ block structure from the genus-$2$
sewing kernel: diagonal = two genus-$1$ Laplacians,
off-diagonal = inter-handle coupling through the Schur
complement $S = K_{q_2} + R_{21}(1 - K_{q_1})^{-1}R_{12}$.
The programme proceeds in four steps.

\emph{Step~1: The $\mathrm{Sp}(4)$ Arthur packet
discrimination.}
Automorphic representations of $\mathrm{Sp}(4)$ have a
richer structure than $\mathrm{GL}(2)$ via Arthur's
classification:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \emph{Stable (tempered) packets}: genuine Siegel
  modular forms, satisfying the Ramanujan conjecture
  (Weissauer, for holomorphic cusp forms of sufficiently
  high weight).
\item \emph{Endoscopic (non-tempered) packets}:
  Saito--Kurokawa lifts from $\mathrm{GL}(2)$, which do
  \emph{not} satisfy the full Ramanujan bound for
  $\mathrm{Sp}(4)$.
\end{itemize}
The key observation: the off-diagonal sewing coupling
$R_{12}, R_{21}$ detects whether a Siegel modular form is
a Saito--Kurokawa lift.  An SK lift factors through the
genus-$1$ sewing (it lifts from $\mathrm{GL}(2)$), so its
off-diagonal coupling is ``reducible.''  A genuine Siegel
eigenform has ``irreducible'' off-diagonal coupling.  The
spectral gap of $\Delta_B^{(2)}$ could force the
representations to lie in the stable (tempered) class,
which \emph{is} the Ramanujan condition.

\emph{Step~2: The B\"ocherer bridge converts to $L$-values.}
The proved B\"ocherer factorisation
(Theorem~\ref{thm:bocherer-bridge}, via
Furusawa--Morimoto/DPSS) gives
\[
  \frac{|B_f(D)|^2}{\langle \mathrm{SK}(f), \mathrm{SK}(f)
  \rangle}
  = \alpha(r,k)\,
  L(\tfrac{1}{2}, \pi_f)\,
  L(\tfrac{1}{2}, \pi_f \times \chi_D).
\]
The genus-$2$ MC data determines the Fourier coefficients
$a_\Lambda(T)$ of the genus-$2$ theta series.  These feed
into the B\"ocherer coefficient~$B_f(D)$.  If the spectral
gap forces the relevant amplitudes to be nonzero (through
an anti-cancellation mechanism), then
$L(\tfrac{1}{2}, \pi_f \times \chi_D) \neq 0$.

\emph{Step~3: Three sub-gaps (honest assessment).}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{Gap \Roman*:},leftmargin=*]
\item \emph{Spectral gap $\to$ amplitude lower bound.}
  A spectral gap provides exponential decay of off-diagonal
  matrix elements, not a lower bound.  The gap controls
  coherence length---it prevents arbitrary cancellation among
  Fourier modes---but converting this to a pointwise lower
  bound on $B_f(D) = \sum_{[T]} a_{\mathrm{cusp}}(T)/\varepsilon(T)$
  requires showing the Schur complement structure prevents
  destructive interference.
\item \emph{Individual vs.\ aggregate positivity.}
  The existing bracket positivity
  (Proposition~\ref{prop:bracket-hodge-index}) gives
  positivity of a \emph{weighted sum} of $L$-values (the
  genus-$2$ Beurling kernel), not individual $L$-values.
  The spectral gap would need to interact with the Petersson
  inner product to isolate individual eigenform contributions.
\item \emph{Planted-forest anti-cancellation.}
  The planted-forest corrections from $\FM_3$ (the cubic
  obstruction) provide the cusp-projection channel.  The Schur
  complement (positive operator $+$ positive correction)
  ensures the total determinant is bounded below.  But
  converting this to non-vanishing of \emph{individual}
  B\"ocherer coefficients is the precise open problem.
\end{enumerate}

\emph{Step~4: Why genus~$2$ might work where genus~$1$
provably fails.}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\arabic*)}]
\item Genus-$1$ structural separation is a \emph{theorem}:
  the MC equation is algebraically exhausted, the RS unfolding
  erases scattering data, the arithmetic content is
  pluriharmonic.
\item Genus-$2$ non-diagonality is \emph{proved}: the
  off-diagonal blocks $R_{12}, R_{21}$ break the
  Fock-diagonal structure.
\item The B\"ocherer channel bypasses RS unfolding
  \emph{entirely}: it extracts $L$-values from Fourier
  coefficients, not from Eisenstein spectral decomposition.
\item $\mathrm{Sp}(4)$ Arthur packets provide the
  discrimination mechanism that $\mathrm{GL}(2)$ lacks: the
  distinction between tempered and non-tempered is precisely
  what the off-diagonal coupling measures.
\item Weissauer's theorem already proves Ramanujan for
  genus-$2$ holomorphic cusp forms using exactly the mechanism
  of detecting SK lifts via their factorisation structure---which
  is what the off-diagonal sewing coupling computes.
\end{enumerate}

\emph{The relationship to RH.}
The genus escalation principle extends to all genera: at
genus~$g$, conjectural B\"ocherer-type formulas access
$L(\tfrac{1}{2}, \mathrm{Sym}^{g-1}f)$.  The Serre reduction
says: non-vanishing of all symmetric power $L$-functions at
$s = \tfrac{1}{2}$ implies the Ramanujan conjecture, which
implies (conditionally) critical-line location of $\zeta$ zeros.
The gaps at higher genus: B\"ocherer-type formulas are
conjectural at $g \geq 3$, and Langlands functoriality
$\mathrm{GL}(2) \to \mathrm{GL}(g)$ is open for $g \geq 6$
(Newton--Thorne resolves it for holomorphic eigenforms of
regular algebraic weight, but not for Maass forms).

\subsection{The algebra--geometry--arithmetic trichotomy}
\label{sec:alg-geom-arith-vol1}

The partition function $Z(\tau)$ is the meeting point of three
mathematical worlds.  The bar complex~$\barB(\cA)$ encodes the
\emph{algebra}: the OPE data that determines~$Z$ as a
$q$-series.  The hyperbolic Laplacian $\Delta_H$ on
$\cM_{1,1} = \mathrm{SL}(2,\bZ)\backslash\mathfrak{H}$
encodes the \emph{geometry}: the spectral theory of the
modular surface whose continuous spectrum is governed by the
scattering matrix $\varphi(s) = \Lambda(1{-}s)/\Lambda(s)$,
with poles at $s = \rho/2$ (the zeta zeros).  The Riemann
zeta function encodes the \emph{arithmetic}: the distribution
of primes, which controls the lattice
$\mathrm{SL}(2,\bZ)$ acting on~$\mathfrak{H}$.

\textbf{The bar complex does not encode the zeta zeros.}  It
encodes the algebra~$\cA$, which produces $Z(\tau)$.  The
zeros are a property of how $Z(\tau)$ \emph{sits on the
modular surface}---they come from the geometry of
$\cM_{1,1}$, not from the algebra of the chiral product.
The chain is:
\[
  \barB(\cA) \;\to\; Z(\tau) \text{ as $q$-series}
  \;\to\; Z(\tau) \text{ on } \cM_{1,1}
  \;\xrightarrow{\Delta_H}\;
  \text{spectral decomposition} \;\ni\; \zeta\text{ zeros}.
\]
The bar complex controls the first arrow; the zeta zeros
enter at the last.

\textbf{What the CFT ``knows.''}  The zeta zeros correspond
to the \emph{equidistribution rate} of $Z(\tau)$ on the
modular surface.  The Eisenstein spectrum controls how fast
a function on~$\cM_{1,1}$ approaches its average.  For
$Z(\tau)$ with $d(n) \geq 0$ (unitarity), the
equidistribution rate is controlled by the spectral gap
$\lambda_1$ of~$\Delta_H$---and this gap \emph{is} the
Selberg eigenvalue conjecture, which \emph{is} the Ramanujan
conjecture for $\mathrm{GL}(2)$ Maass forms.

The CFT does not ``know'' where the zeros are through its
algebraic structure.  It ``knows'' through its
\emph{equidistribution rate on the moduli space}---a property
requiring the full analytic structure of $Z(\tau)$ as a
function on a hyperbolic surface, not just its $q$-expansion
coefficients.  The bar complex gives you the $q$-series; what
you need is the hyperbolic geometry of the function that
$q$-series defines.

See Volume~II, \S\ref*{sec:algebra-geometry-arithmetic},
for the full development.

\subsection{The Koszul fixed point and the zeta fixed point}

\begin{proposition}[The two fixed points are unrelated]
\label{prop:two-fixed-points}
The Koszul fixed point $c = 13$ and the critical line
$\mathrm{Re}(s) = 1/2$ arise from different structures:
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\roman*)}]
\item $c = 13$ is the fixed point of $c \mapsto 26 - c$ (the
  Koszul involution on the Virasoro family).  It is determined
  by $c + c' = 26$ (Freudenthal--de~Vries), which depends on
  the Virasoro central charge formula $c = 1 - 6(p-q)^2/(pq)$
  and the Sugawara construction.  This is CFT data.
\item $\mathrm{Re}(s) = 1/2$ is the fixed line of
  $s \mapsto 1 - s$ (the functional equation of~$\zeta$).  It is
  determined by the Euler product and the gamma factors.  This
  is number-theoretic data.
\end{enumerate}
The coincidence $c/2 = 13/2$ and $s = 1/2$ share the symbol
``$1/2$'' but arise from unrelated arithmetic.  The critical
line of $\zeta$ is a consequence of the functional equation
$\xi(s) = \xi(1-s)$; the self-duality of $\mathrm{Vir}_{13}$
is a consequence of $26 - 13 = 13$.  No mechanism connects them.
\end{proposition}

The temptation to identify $c = 13$ with $s = 1/2$ is a
special case of the meta-principle (AP9): the same name (``fixed
point of an involution'') applied to different objects does not
make them the same.

\subsection{What would close the chain?}

To close the descent chain, one would need a theorem of the
following form:

\begin{conjecture}[Closed descent, if true]
\label{conj:closed-descent}
There exists a natural transformation
$\Phi\colon \mathrm{ShTow}(\cA) \to S_\cA$ from the shadow obstruction tower
functor to the Dirichlet-sewing lift, such that:
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\roman*)}]
\item $\Phi$ intertwines the Galois involution $\sigma$ of
  $K_L$ with the operation $\cA \mapsto \cA^!$ on $S_\cA$.
\item The functional equation of $S_\cA(u)$ descends from the
  complementarity identity
  $\Delta(\cA) + \Delta(\cA^!) = 6960/[(5c{+}22)(152{-}5c)]$.
\item The Euler product of $S_\cA(u)$ (when it exists) factors
  through the shadow spectral decomposition.
\end{enumerate}
\end{conjecture}

No such natural transformation is known.  The obstruction is
at step (i): the shadow obstruction tower lives over the function field
$\Q(c)(t)$ (a quadratic extension in the arity variable~$t$),
while the Dirichlet-sewing lift lives over the complex $u$-plane
(the spectral variable).  The two variables parametrise different
structures: $t$ is the arity, $u$ is the Mellin conjugate of
the eigenvalue $q^n$ of the sewing operator.  To connect them
would require identifying the arity expansion of $\sqrt{Q_L}$
with the Mellin transform of the sewing determinant, and no such
identification exists.

\subsection{Is the claim ``they are the same involution'' correct?}

No.

The descent chain contains three distinct involutions:
\begin{enumerate}[nosep]
\item \textbf{Verdier duality} $\bD_{\Ran}$ on $\Ran(X)$:
  exchanges $\B(\cA)$ and $\B(\cA^!)$.  Acts on factorisation
  (co)algebras.  This is the Fourier involution.
\item \textbf{Galois involution} $\sigma$ of $K_L/F$: sends
  $\sqrt{Q_L} \mapsto -\sqrt{Q_L}$.  Acts on the shadow
  extension.  This is the monodromy of $\nabla^{\mathrm{sh}}$,
  intrinsic to a single algebra.
\item \textbf{Koszul involution} $\cA \mapsto \cA^!$: sends
  $c \mapsto 26-c$ (for Virasoro), $k \mapsto -k - 2h^\vee$
  (for Kac--Moody).  Acts on the space of algebras.
\end{enumerate}

These three involutions are all of order~$2$.  They are all
related to the bar construction.  But they act on different
objects, in different categories, with different fixed points:
\begin{center}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}lll@{}}
\textbf{Involution} & \textbf{Acts on} & \textbf{Fixed point} \\
\hline
Verdier & $\mathrm{FactCoalg}(\Ran(X))$ & self-dual coalgebras \\
Galois & $K_L$ & $F$ (the base field) \\
Koszul & families of chiral algebras & self-dual algebras ($c=13$, etc.)
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The claim ``they are the SAME involution (Fourier) acting at
different levels'' conflates these three.  The correct statement:

\begin{observation}[The three involutions]
\label{obs:three-involutions}
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\roman*)}, nosep]
\item The Verdier duality is a theorem (Theorem~A).
\item The Galois involution of the shadow extension is a
  consequence of $\sqrt{Q_L}$ being algebraic of degree~$2$
  (the MC equation forces a quadratic, not a higher-degree,
  algebraic function).
\item The Koszul involution is a structure on the moduli of
  chiral algebras, not on the bar construction itself.
\item Items (i) and (iii) are related: Verdier duality
  intertwines $\B(\cA)$ with $\B(\cA^!)$, so the Koszul
  involution is the ``shadow on moduli'' of Verdier duality.
\item Items (ii) and (iii) are related: the complementarity
  identity constrains $\Delta(\cA)$ and $\Delta(\cA^!)$.
\item Items (i) and (ii) are \emph{not directly related}:
  the Galois involution is intrinsic to a single algebra
  and its shadow obstruction tower, while Verdier duality requires
  a pair $(\cA, \cA^!)$.
\end{enumerate}
The three involutions form a commutative diagram at the
self-dual point, but they are three distinct morphisms in
three distinct categories.
\end{observation}

\subsection{The two-critical-line phenomenon}

The shadow--spectral correspondence
(Theorem~\ref{thm:shadow-spectral-correspondence}) establishes
that for lattice VOAs, the number of critical lines of
$\varepsilon^c_s$ equals the shadow depth minus~$1$.  The
two-critical-line phenomenon ($E_8$: lines at $\mathrm{Re}(s) = 1/2$
and $\mathrm{Re}(s) = 7/2$) is a feature, not an obstruction.
It reflects the Eisenstein-only Hecke decomposition
$\Theta_{E_8} = E_4$: no cusp form, so no third critical line.

The passage from one critical line (rank $\leq 2$, Eisenstein
only) to multiple critical lines (rank $\geq 8$, Eisenstein plus
cuspidal) is the arithmetic face of the transition from class~G/L
to class~M: the shadow obstruction tower acquires arithmetic depth when the
theta function has cuspidal components.

Whether the two-critical-line structure of $E_8$ is an
``obstruction'' depends on what one is trying to prove.  For the
shadow--spectral correspondence, it is confirming evidence.  For a
hypothetical ``shadow RH'' that would force all zeros onto a single
line, it is a definitive refutation: $E_8$ has zeros on two distinct
lines, so no single-critical-line hypothesis can hold for all
modular Koszul algebras.

\subsection{Summary}

The descent chain is a sequence of independently interesting
structures that share a common origin in the bar construction.
The first two links (bar = Fourier, shadow obstruction tower = quadratic
extension) are theorems.  The claim that ``they are the same
involution at different levels'' is false: there are three
distinct involutions.  The link from the shadow obstruction tower to the
Dirichlet-sewing lift is broken: these are independent invariants.
The link from the Dirichlet-sewing lift to the zeros of $L$-functions
is proved for lattice algebras and structurally obstructed for
non-lattice algebras.

What the bar construction does provide: a single algebraic object
($\Theta_\cA \in \mathrm{MC}(\gAmod)$) whose finite-order
projections simultaneously produce the shadow obstruction tower (an arithmetic
invariant), the genus tower (a topological invariant), and the
Koszul dual (an algebraic invariant).  These three outputs are
facets of $\Theta_\cA$, not levels of a single descent.  The
correct picture is a \emph{fan}, not a \emph{chain}: three
projections from one object, not five levels of one reduction.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzcd}[row sep=1.5em, column sep=2em]
  & \Theta_\cA \ar[dl] \ar[d] \ar[dr] & \\
  \text{Shadow obstruction tower} & \text{Genus tower} & \text{Koszul dual}\\
  \text{(arithmetic)} & \text{(topological)} & \text{(algebraic)}
\end{tikzcd}
\end{center}


% =================================================================
\section{What the machine computes and what it does not}
\label{sec:honest-scope}
% =================================================================

The preceding thirty-five sections build a single machine.  The
input is a chiral algebra $\cA$: its OPE coefficients, its
conformal weights, its central charge.  The output is a cascade
of invariants: five theorems, a shadow obstruction tower, a depth
classification, a genus expansion, a shadow metric, a quadratic
extension, an Epstein zeta function.

This section says what the machine computes, what it does not,
and where the boundary lies.

\subsection{What the machine computes}

Seven outputs.  Each is proved, universal on its domain, and
admits no caveats beyond the hypotheses stated.

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(a) The shadow obstruction tower.}\enspace
For any chirally Koszul algebra $\cA$ with OPE data
$(\kappa, \alpha, S_4)$, the shadow obstruction tower $\{S_r\}_{r \geq 2}$
is the sequence of Taylor coefficients
\begin{equation}\label{eq:shadow-tower-summary}
S_r \;=\; \frac{1}{r}\,[t^{r-2}]\,\sqrt{Q_L(t)},
\qquad
Q_L(t) = (2\kappa + 3\alpha t)^2 + 2\Delta\, t^2,
\quad \Delta = 8\kappa S_4.
\end{equation}
Each $S_r$ is a rational function of the structure constants.
The generating function $H(t) = t^2\sqrt{Q_L(t)}$ is algebraic
of degree~$2$.  Proved: the Riccati-type identity
$H^2 = t^4 Q_L$ is the MC equation projected to each arity
(\S\ref{sec:one-quadratic-form}).  Computed: $580$ exact
rational shadow coefficients across $15$ algebras to arity~$30$
(\S\ref{sec:tables}), verified by three independent
implementations (\S\ref{sec:three-roads}).

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(b) The depth classification.}\enspace
Every chirally Koszul algebra falls into one of four classes,
determined by the critical discriminant $\Delta = 8\kappa S_4$:

\begin{center}
\small
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
\begin{tabular}{@{}clcl@{}}
\textbf{Class} & \textbf{Criterion} & $r_{\max}$ & \textbf{Examples} \\
\hline
G & $\alpha = \Delta = 0$ & $2$ & Heisenberg \\
L & $\alpha \neq 0$, $\Delta = 0$ & $3$ & affine KM \\
C & $\kappa = 0$ (stratum separation) & $4$ & $\beta\gamma$ \\
M & $\Delta \neq 0$ & $\infty$ & Virasoro, $\cW_N$
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\noindent
Proved: the splitting behaviour of $Q_L$ forces
$r_{\max} \in \{2, 3, 4, \infty\}$ with no other values
(Proposition~\ref{prop:shadow-splitting}).  Verified
computationally for all standard families through arity~$20$.

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(c) The shadow connection.}\enspace
The logarithmic connection
$\nabla^{\mathrm{sh}} = d - Q_L'/(2Q_L)\,dt$ has simple poles
at the zeros of $Q_L$, residue~$1/2$ at each zero, and
monodromy~$-1$: the Koszul sign.  The flat sections are
$\sqrt{Q_L(t)/Q_L(0)}$.  The Galois group of the shadow
extension $K_L = F(\sqrt{Q_L})$ is $\Z/2$.  Proved by direct
computation (\S\ref{sec:one-quadratic-form},
\S\ref{sec:arithmetic-shadow-tower}).  The complementarity
identity
$\Delta(\cA) + \Delta(\cA^!) = 6960/[(5c{+}22)(152{-}5c)]$
constrains the discriminant pair with a universal numerator
$6960 = 2^4 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 \cdot 29$.

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(d) The genus expansion.}\enspace
For uniform-weight modular Koszul algebras:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:genus-expansion-summary}
F_g(\cA) \;=\; \kappa(\cA) \cdot \lambda_g^{\mathrm{FP}},
\qquad
\sum_{g \geq 1} \lambda_g^{\mathrm{FP}}\, \hbar^{2g}
= \hat{A}(i\hbar) - 1.
\end{equation}
The $\hat{A}$-genus generates the tower from a single scalar.
At all genera.  The bar propagator $d\log E(z,w)$ has
weight~$1$ regardless of the conformal weight of the field
(\S\ref{sec:what-it-becomes}); all edge-level Hodge data is
standard.  Genus-$1$ universality $F_1 = \kappa/24$ is proved
unconditionally for all families.  The decisive test:
$F_2(V_{E_8}) = 7/1440$ matches the Siegel--Weil formula
(\S\ref{sec:decisive-test}).

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(e) The bar-cobar machine.}\enspace
Five main theorems.
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*),nosep]
\item \textbf{Theorem A} (adjunction):
  $\bD_{\Ran}(\B(\cA)) \simeq \B(\cA^!)$.  Bar intertwines
  convolution with pointwise.
\item \textbf{Theorem B} (inversion):
  $\Omega(\B(\cA)) \xrightarrow{\sim} \cA$ on the Koszul locus.
\item \textbf{Theorem C} (complementarity):
  $Q_g(\cA) \oplus Q_g(\cA^!) \cong H^*(\overline\cM_g, \cZ_\cA)$.
\item \textbf{Theorem D} (modular characteristic):
  $\mathrm{obs}_g = \kappa(\cA) \cdot \lambda_g$ for
  uniform-weight algebras.
\item \textbf{Theorem H} (Hochschild):
  $\ChirHoch^*(\cA)$ polynomial, Koszul-functorial.
\end{enumerate}
All proved.  Together with the Koszulness programme
(eight genuinely independent bidirectional equivalences plus one
one-way chiral Hochschild consequence, one conditional Lagrangian
criterion, one one-directional D-module purity), the MC element
$\Theta_\cA = D_\cA - d_0$,
and the all-genera sewing theorem.

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(f) The Epstein zeta.}\enspace
For rational $c$, the shadow metric $Q_L$ defines an Epstein
zeta function $\varepsilon^c_s$ with a functional equation
$\Lambda(s) = \Lambda(c/2{+}1{-}s)$.  The specialised shadow
field $K(c) = \Q(\sqrt{d(c)})$ is a specific quadratic number
field at each rational central charge.  Proved
(\S\ref{sec:specialisation-functor}).  Computed: the Ising
model lives over $\Q(\sqrt{-10})$; the self-dual point
$c = 13$ over $\Q(\sqrt{-435})$; the Koszul pair $c = 1$ and
$c = 25$ over the same field $\Q(\sqrt{-15})$.

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(g) The gravitational coproduct.}\enspace
For any principal DS reduction, the ghost-number
obstruction kills the bar coproduct:
$\varepsilon \circ \Delta^{\mathrm{grav}} = 0$ at all arities.
The genus expansion of the reduced algebra inherits the DS
cascade: shadow depth increases from class~L to class~M
(\S\ref{sec:depth-creation}).  Verified for $N = 2, \ldots, 5$
to arity~$20$.

\subsection{What the machine does not compute}

Six things were attempted or hoped for.  Each fails, and the
failure is structural.

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(h) Universal Langlands.}\enspace
The chain
$\cA \to S_\cA(u) \to \text{functional equation} \to
\text{automorphic representation}$
does not close universally.  Three obstructions:

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\roman*),nosep]
\item The Dirichlet-sewing lift $S_\cA(u)$ is defined from the
  weight multiset $W(\cA) = \{w_i\}$, which is genus-$0$ data
  (\S\ref{sec:prime-factorisation}).  The Koszul involution
  $c \mapsto 26 - c$ acts on the shadow extension, not on
  $S_\cA(u)$: the lift forgets the central charge.
\item For Virasoro ($W = \{2\}$):
  $S_{\mathrm{Vir}}(u) = \zeta(u{+}1)(\zeta(u) - 1)$. The
  subtracted harmonic polynomial breaks the Euler product.  At
  weight $\geq 2$, no prime factorisation.
\item Route C (automorphic counting) requires finitely many
  spectral atoms.  Class~M has $r_{\max} = \infty$.
\end{enumerate}

The shadow obstruction tower and the Dirichlet-sewing lift are logically
independent: the tower is determined by $(\kappa, \alpha, S_4)$;
the lift by $W(\cA)$.  Two algebras with the same weight
multiset but different OPE coefficients have the same
$S_\cA(u)$ but different shadow obstruction towers
(Warning~\ref{warn:dirichlet-not-shadow}).  The link at
Level~$2 \to 3$ of the descent chain is broken
(\S\ref{sec:descent-chain}).

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(i) Non-lattice $L$-functions beyond
Epstein.}\enspace
The Epstein zeta function $\varepsilon^c_s$ gives an
$L$-function for the shadow quadratic field.  The automorphic
$L$-function (the object connected to the Ramanujan
conjecture) requires additional structure: an Euler product,
$\mathrm{GL}(n)$ twists, the CPS converse theorem.  None is
available for non-lattice algebras.

For lattice vertex algebras, the Hecke decomposition
$\Theta_L = E_{d/2} + \sum_f c_f \cdot f$ factors
$\varepsilon^c_s$ into products of classical $L$-functions
(\S\ref{sec:arithmetic-shadow-tower}).
Unconditional Ramanujan for lattice VOAs follows by Deligne.
But this mechanism depends on the theta function being a
modular form of level~$1$ with Hecke eigenvalues---structure
that non-lattice algebras do not possess.

The MC equation constrains the spectral coefficients on the
real axis $s = 1/2 + it$.  The nontrivial zeros of $\zeta$
live at complex~$t$.  The gap is structural: OPE data is
finite-dimensional; the zeros of an entire function of
order~$1$ encode infinitely many independent quantities
(Warning~\ref{warn:no-shadow-rh}).

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(j) A single descent.}\enspace
The three involutions (Verdier duality on $\Ran(X)$, Galois
involution of $K_L/F$, Koszul involution $\cA \mapsto \cA^!$)
are three distinct $\Z/2$-actions on three distinct objects
(Observation~\ref{obs:three-involutions}).  The claim ``they
are levels of a single Fourier duality'' is false.  The Verdier
involution acts on factorisation coalgebras.  The Galois
involution acts on the shadow extension.  The Koszul involution
acts on the moduli of chiral algebras.  They commute at the
self-dual point $c = 13$ but do not coincide.

The correct picture is a fan, not a chain:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzcd}[row sep=1.5em, column sep=2em]
  & \Theta_\cA \ar[dl] \ar[d] \ar[dr] & \\
  \text{Shadow obstruction tower} & \text{Genus tower} & \text{Koszul dual}
\end{tikzcd}
\end{center}

\noindent
Three independent projections from one MC element.  Not five
levels of one reduction.

\medskip

\noindent\textbf{(k) Multi-generator universality at
$g \geq 2$.}\enspace
The formula $\mathrm{obs}_g = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g$ is proved
for uniform-weight algebras (all generators have the same
